    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="c909c4fd95554ed786d81182a2e12506">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'bfe19334-df58-405e-b8d8-7835003cabad'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d8816143" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>The inherent high reactive oxygen-functional groups of hard carbon (HC) anode are strategically converted into a stable Si−O−Si molecular layer for regulating uncontrollable interfacial redox reaction energy and charge transfer, suppressing the interfacial side reactions, and contributing to the construction of inorganic/organic hybrid solid electrolyte interphase with low interfacial resistance and enhanced cycling durability.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/616bf21c-8b8d-4767-a023-2db76933629e/anie202424028-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/616bf21c-8b8d-4767-a023-2db76933629e/anie202424028-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a44efd36-7441-417c-9ca2-eb872fa2cf44/anie202424028-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/616bf21c-8b8d-4767-a023-2db76933629e/anie202424028-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d8816146" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Commercial hard carbon (HC) anode suffers from unexpected interphase chemistry rooted in the parasitic reactions between surface oxygen-functional groups and ester-based electrolytes. Herein, an innovative strategy is proposed to regulate interphase chemistry by tailoring targeted functional groups on the HC surface, where highly active undesirable oxygen-functional groups are skillfully converted into a Si−O−Si molecular layer favorable for anchoring anions. Then, an inorganic/organic hybrid solid electrolyte interphase with low interfacial charge transfer resistance and enhanced cycling durability is constructed successfully. Consequently, the modified HC anode delivers an excellent rate capability of 206.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and a remarkable capacity retention of 92.5 % after 1000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, the coin-type full-cell equipped with Na<sub>2</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] cathode exhibits an exceptional capacity retention ratio of 80.9 % after 800 cycles at 1C. The present simple and effective interfacial modification strategy offers a promising and alternative avenue for promoting the development and practicability of HC anode in ester-based electrolytes for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202424028-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202424028-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are widely considered an effective complement to lithium-ion batteries due to their acceptable energy density, cost-effectiveness, and abundant resources.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0001_R_d8816135e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Hard carbon (HC) is regarded as the most promising anode material of SIBs owing to the merits of low cost and high theoretical specific capacity (&gt;300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0005_R_d8816135e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> However, the actual capacity of the HC anode is regulated not only by the inherent properties of HC materials including crystallinity, defects, and nano-pore but also by the electrochemical properties of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0004_R_d8816135e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1c - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">1c</a>, <a href="#anie202424028-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0008_R_d8816135e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> As a special interphase between the anode and the electrolyte, the SEI directly dominates interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics and interfacial stability, which is closely related to the actual capacity of HC. Unfortunately, unexpected interphase chemistry between the HC anode and commercialized ester-based electrolytes triggers the unstable SEI layer and struggling interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> migration behavior, ultimately leading to low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), poor rate capability, and inferior cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0011_R_d8816135e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Notably, understanding the interaction between surface functional groups of HC and electrolyte components is the premise for regulating interphase chemistry. Concretely, HC material is obtained by calcining biomass or anthracite, which inevitably leads to the residual of some oxygen-functional groups on its surface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0012_R_d8816135e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> Commonly, these oxygen-functional groups present several challenges:<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0015_R_d8816135e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> 1) They are prone to adsorbing and catalyzing highly polar ester-based solvent molecules, which increases interfacial side reactions and deteriorates the electrolytes. 2) During the construction of the SEI, the decomposition fragments of ester-based solvent molecules (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>, CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup>, etc.) are easily captured by these oxygen-functional groups, making the SEI layer thick and uneven, which seriously hinders the interfacial transport of Na<sup>+</sup>. 3) The repeated formation and reconstruction of SEI cause electrolyte consumption and irreversible capacity loss. These uncontrolled interfacial redox reaction energy and charges transfer between HC anode and electrolytes seriously hazard the stable operation of the SEI. Therefore, it is crucial for the HC anode in ester-based electrolytes to regulate the surface oxygen-functional groups of the HC anode to form a stable and robust SEI.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we propose a novel method to regulate the interphase chemistry of HC anode in ester-based electrolytes. In particular, a Si−O−Si molecular layer is skillfully bridged on the surface of the HC anode via chemical bonds between trimethoxysilane (TMS) molecules and oxygen-functional groups of the HC (hereafter TMS-HC). The inherently highly active oxygen-functional groups on the HC surface are ingeniously converted into targeted functional groups that can specifically anchor anions. The enhanced ion-dipole interactions between targeted functional groups and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> promote the enrichment of anions at the HC anode/electrolytes interface, directionally guiding the formation of an inorganic/organic hybrid SEI. In addition, the uniformly distributed inorganic and organic species in SEI significantly enhance the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> and the long-term stability of the interphase, contributing to a comprehensive promotion of sodium-storage performance for the HC anode. Consequently, both the Na || TMS-HC half-cell and the TMS-HC || Na<sub>2</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] (Fe-PB) full-cell demonstrate remarkably improved electrochemical performance. Moreover, the practical model of TMS-HC anode in pouch-cell and its convenient large-scale preparation highlights its attractive option for commercial application.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202424028-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202424028-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202424028-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202424028-sec-0003-title"> Targeted Functionalization of HC Surface</h3>
            
            <p>The formation process of targeted functionalization of the HC surface is illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">1a</a>. Concretely, the TMS will form silanols after hydrolysis, and the hydroxyl groups on the silanols will spontaneously react with oxygen-functional groups (hydroxyl, carboxyl, etc.) on the HC surface to form chemical bonds. Meanwhile, silanol molecules also dehydrate and condense to form a Si−O−Si molecular layer. Consequently, the inherent oxygen-functional groups with strong reactivity are depleted and the Si−O−Si molecular layer with targeted functional groups effectively covers the surface of the HC anode. Furthermore, owing to the strong ion-dipole interaction between PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and targeted functional groups in the Si−O−Si molecular layer, the anions can be enriched on the surface of HC to regulate the composition and structure of the SEI.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202424028-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b483a072-0d98-4542-b270-f0faec77d402/anie202424028-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b483a072-0d98-4542-b270-f0faec77d402/anie202424028-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ffe24fbe-c63e-4617-98ed-d556426f1bf0/anie202424028-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b483a072-0d98-4542-b270-f0faec77d402/anie202424028-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202424028-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202424028" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Theoretical calculations and physical characterization of targeted functional groups. (a) Schematic illustration of the formation process of targeted functionalization of HC surface. (b) The FT-IR spectra of the surface of HC and TMS-HC. (c) Si 2p XPS spectra of TMS-HC. TEM images of HC (d) and TMS-HC (e). Snapshots of MD simulations of HC/electrolyte system (f) and TMS-HC/electrolyte system (g) (Grey: C; Red: O; White: H; Blue: P; Purple: Na; Pink: P; Yellow: Si). Density distribution of solvent molecules (O) and anions (F) in HC system (h) and TMS-HC system (i). (j) Binding energies of the EC, PC, EMC, and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> with functional groups of HC and TMS-HC.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Multiple techniques are performed to illustrate that the Si−O−Si molecular layer is constructed successfully. Figure S1 shows that O and Si elements are present and evenly distributed. The clear peaks of Si−O−Si (835 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1074 cm<sup>−1</sup>), Si−O−C (1130 cm<sup>−1</sup>), and Si−H (2250 cm<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">1b</a>) in Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra and the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis results of two peaks at 103.9 eV (Si−O−Si) and 102.9 eV (Si−O−C) in the Si 2p spectra (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">1c</a> and S2) demonstrate the chemical bridging between the molecular layer and the HC surface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0016_R_d8816135e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a>, <a href="#anie202424028-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0019_R_d8816135e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a></sup></span> It can be observed that the thickness of the molecular layer is about 8.1 nm by high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">1d</a> and <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">e</a>). In addition, the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value of TMS-HC (1.01) is smaller than that of HC (1.09), indicating the defects on the surface of TMS-HC being covered by the Si−O−Si molecular layer (Figure S3).<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0020_R_d8816135e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>To further understand the microscopic behaviors of solvent molecules and anions on the surface of HC and TMS-HC anode, MD simulations are performed (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">1f</a> and <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">g</a>). For the HC surface, the position of the carbon layer can be determined by the density distribution of element C at 4.5 Å (Figure S4). Evidently, solvent molecules accumulate on the surface of the HC anode while anions are far away (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">1h</a>). For the TMS-HC surface, the content of anions on the surface of TMS-HC significantly increases (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">1i</a> and S5). Specifically, it can be observed that there are a certain amount of anions and solvent molecules between the TMS-HC surface and the Si−O−Si molecular layer (Figure S5, 5.5 Å), indicating the electrolyte components can penetrate the molecular layer. Furthermore, the correlation between surface functional groups and the electrolyte components by the density functional theory (DFT) calculations (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">1j</a>). The binding energies between EC, PC, EMC, and -OH of the HC surface are relatively −0.23 eV, −0.19 eV, and −0.23 eV. While the binding energies between EC, PC, EMC, and −O−Si−H (Reaction products of -OH and TMS) of the TMS-HC surface significantly decreased (−0.16 eV, −0.16 eV and −0.13 eV, relatively). Similarly, the interaction of -COOH (HC surface) on solvent molecules is also weaker than that of −CO−Si−H (TMS-HC surface) on solvent molecules. Specifically, a greater force can be applied to PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> on the surface of TMS-HC, indicating the existence of strong ion-dipole interaction between PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and targeted functional groups in the Si−O−Si molecular layer. These results strongly demonstrate the effective construction of the Si−O−Si molecular layer and its superiority in regulating interphase chemistry.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202424028-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202424028-sec-0004-title"> Sodium-Storage Performance of TMS-HC Anode</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance is investigated by galvanostatic charging/discharging (GCD) measurements. The TMS-HC performs a higher ICE (82.6 %) than the HC (75.9 %) (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0002">2a</a>), owing to the consumption of high reactive oxygen-functional groups. Notably, the slope capacity of TMS-HC (167.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) is lower than that of HC (233.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) during the first charging process, indicating that the Si−O−Si molecular layer can reduce the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> at the surface and edge due to the consumption of surface oxygen functional groups and the concealment of defects.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202424028-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c4f6fe04-7d16-4d71-8218-5ef49f0f8dc9/anie202424028-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c4f6fe04-7d16-4d71-8218-5ef49f0f8dc9/anie202424028-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ef699007-b2eb-4fd4-bca7-5fb1a9440c05/anie202424028-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c4f6fe04-7d16-4d71-8218-5ef49f0f8dc9/anie202424028-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202424028-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202424028" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performance of HC and TMS-HC anode. (a) GCD curves of the initial cycle. dQ/dV plots upon cycling for the HC anode (b) and TMS-HC anode (c). (d) Long-term cycling performance at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (e) Normalized contribution ratio of sloping region capacity and plateau region capacity at different cycles of TMS-HC. (f) Rate performance. (g) Long-term cycling performance of TMS-HC anode at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (h) Comparison of reversible capacity and rate performance of the TMS-HC anode with reported HC anode.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Furthermore, the durability and stability are verified by long cycling performance. Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0002">2b</a> and <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0002">c</a> show the dQ/dV plots at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for the first 100 cycles of HC and TMS-HC, respectively. Compared to the dQ/dV curves of HC, the intensity of the dQ/dV curves of TMS-HC is more stable, indicating that the cycling stability of the TMS-HC anode is greatly enhanced. The same results can be observed from the GCD curves of HC and TMS-HC anode ranging from 1 to 100 cycles (Figure S6). Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0002">2d</a> shows the long-cycling performance of HC and TMS-HC at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The HC anode exhibits an unacceptable specific capacity of 187.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles. In contrast, the TMS-HC anode still exhibits a high specific capacity of 266.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles, demonstrating exceptional durability, primarily attributed to the robust and homogeneous SEI. Compared to the HC anode, the TMS-HC anode shows an almost invariant capacity contribution percentage in the slope and plateau regions with increasing cycles, revealing the stable Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption and intercalation behavior (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0002">2e</a> and S7).</p>
            
            <p>The rate capability of HC and TMS-HC are tested after two cycles at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> to evaluate their application potential, and the results are shown in Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0002">2f</a> and S8. Obviously, the TMS-HC exhibits more outstanding specific capacity than that of HC, possessing a specific capacity two times that of HC at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0002">2g</a>, the long cycling life of TMS-HC at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> is further evaluated. Owing to the robust and stable anion-derived SEI, TMS-HC achieves a high specific capacity of 95.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an excellent capacity retention of 92.5 % after 1000 cycles. The TMS-HC anode also exhibits better performance than that of the HC anode in ether-based electrolytes (Figure S9). Surprisingly, the performance of the TMS-HC anode is superior to that of previously reported HC anodes (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0002">2h</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0021_R_d8816135e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202424028-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202424028-sec-0005-title"> Interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> Transport and Charges Transfer Kinetic</h3>
            
            <p>Operando electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) is operated to understand the formation properties of the SEI during the initial charging/discharging process (Figure S10), and the EIS spectra are interpreted by the distribution of relaxation times (DRT) model (Figures S11, 3a, and 3b). Visibly, compared with the Na || HC half-cell, the lower R<sub>ct</sub> and R<sub>SEI</sub> and more uniform impedance distribution of Na || TMS-HC half-cell imply the enhanced stability of the SEI and the promoted Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion through SEI, further demonstrated by the lower values of peaks in the relaxation time range of 10<sup>−2</sup>–100 s and 10<sup>−4</sup>–10<sup>−2</sup> s in 2D intensity color map of the DRT curves.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0027_R_d8816135e823" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> Consequently, the improved SEI and promoted charge transfer are confirmed.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The electrochemical behavior and kinetic differences of Na<sup>+</sup> of HC and TMS-HC anode with ester-based electrolyte are further researched. Figure S12 exhibits the peak potential in cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of HC and TMS-HC as the scanning rate increases. Here, the variation of |<i>E<sub>Pa</sub></i>−<i>E<sub>Pc</sub></i>| as the scanning rate increases is used to evaluate the reversibility of the anode, where <i>E<sub>Pa</sub></i> is the peak potential of oxidation and <i>E<sub>Pc</sub></i> is the peak potential of reduction. Obviously, the variation of |<i>E<sub>Pa</sub></i>−<i>E<sub>Pc</sub></i>| for TMS-HC is lower than that of HC as the scanning rate increases (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0003">3c</a>), demonstrating that the targeted functional groups enhance the cycling stability of the anode. Typically, the peak current (<i>i</i>) and scanning rate (<i>v</i>) meet the following equations:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202424028-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7b829499-3b05-4135-b527-96f9177e7dda/anie202424028-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               
               <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202424028-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/19bc5005-e269-44c6-b10e-867aa69aef2d/anie202424028-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202424028-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7c94318d-a2da-4cc5-8184-0ad47c774ba0/anie202424028-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7c94318d-a2da-4cc5-8184-0ad47c774ba0/anie202424028-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/42a74bf5-6abf-4e39-9661-75d79099b350/anie202424028-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7c94318d-a2da-4cc5-8184-0ad47c774ba0/anie202424028-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202424028-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202424028" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Interfacial kinetics evolution upon the formation of the SEI. Two-dimensional intensity color maps of the DRT curves for (a) Na || HC half-cell and (b) Na || TMS-HC half-cell at the first charging-discharging process. (c) Plots of |E<sub>pa</sub>−E<sub>pc</sub>| versus v for CV curves. (d) Plots of log(i) versus log(v) and the corresponding linear fitting. (e) R<sub>SEI</sub> of Na || HC and Na || TMS-HC half-cell at different temperatures. (f) R<sub>ct</sub> of Na || HC and Na || TMS-HC half-cell at different temperatures. (g) The activation energies of Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the SEI. (h) The activation energies for the charge-transfer process. <i>D</i><sub><i>Na+</i></sub> of HC and TMS-HC anodes calculated from GITT potential profiles for the discharging (i) and charging (j) processes.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are the adjustable parameters. If the value of <i>b</i> is close to 1, the electrochemical process is surface-controlled. As the <i>b</i> value is close to 0.5, the electrochemical reaction mainly relies on a diffusion-controlled process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0007_R_d8816135e981" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2b - Adv. Mater.">2b</a></sup></span> It can be calculated that the <i>b</i> values of HC and TMS-HC are 0.70/0.47 (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0003">3d</a>), respectively. The results demonstrate that the electrochemical reaction of the HC anode is controlled by surface and diffusion together, while the electrochemical reaction of TMS-HC is almost solely controlled by diffusion. This transition of the electrochemical behavior of the TMS-HC anode originates from the reduction of Na<sup>+</sup> active sites on the surface caused by the construction of the molecular layer, which can be confirmed by the results of Raman spectra (Figure S3) and initial GCD curves (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0002">2a</a>).</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">To reflect the rapid transport dynamics of Na<sup>+</sup> at the anode/electrolyte interface, the Na<sup>+</sup> transport across the SEI and charge-transfer impedance (R<sub>SEI</sub> and R<sub>ct</sub>, relatively) at different temperatures are further measured by EIS. The EIS values are fitted by the corresponding equivalent circuit (Figures S13 and S14). Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0003">3e</a> and <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0003">f</a> show the R<sub>SEI</sub> and R<sub>ct</sub> of TMS-HC are far lower than that of HC at 20 °C and below, indicating that the interfacial properties of TMS-HC anode/electrolytes have greatly improved. The activation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the anode/electrolytes interface can be quantified by Arrhenius equations:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202424028-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9fbf2ab3-bef4-4ef3-bc44-ce7e0c6c7d18/anie202424028-math-0003.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <p>Where <i>T</i>, <i>R<sub>EIS</sub></i>, <i>R</i>, and <i>A</i> represent absolute temperature, impedance, standard gas constant, and preexponential constant, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0026_R_d8816135e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10e - Adv. Energy Mater.">10e</a>, <a href="#anie202424028-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0030_R_d8816135e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>Based on EIS data at different temperatures, the activation energy of HC and TMS-HC is calculated and shown in Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0003">3g</a> and <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0003">h</a>. Benefitting from the thin and robust SEI with high ionic conductivity, the activation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the SEI (<i>E<sub>SEI</sub></i>) of TMS-HC is 16.38 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, which is significantly smaller than that of HC (28.85 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>). The activation energy of charge-transport (<i>E<sub>ct</sub></i>) of TMS-HC (37.16 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) is also smaller than that of HC (40.90 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>). The greatly decreased energy barrier proves that the Si−O−Si molecular layer is important in improving Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and SEI durability. In addition, the Na<sup>+</sup>-diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub><i>Na+</i></sub>) can be obtained from the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) potential profiles (Figure S15) to further analysis of the superiority of the HC-TMS. Similar <i>D</i><sub><i>Na+</i></sub> indicates that the construction of the self-assembled molecular layer can hardly affect the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> between carbon layers in the HC-TMS anode (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0003">3i</a> and <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0003">j</a>).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202424028-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202424028-sec-0006-title"> Interphase Chemistry</h3>
            
            <p>The micromorphology of the SEI is an important reflection of SEI regulation, which is closely related to the cycling stability of the anode. The morphology of the SEI on the surface of HC and TMS-HC is investigated by HR-TEM and Atomic Force Microscope (AFM). Figure S16 depicts the TMS-HC surface is more uniform and smoother than that of the HC surface. Meanwhile, compared with Young's modulus of the SEI formed on the HC surface (1.3 GPa, Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0004">4a</a> and S17a), Young's modulus of the SEI formed on the TMS-HC surface is higher (2.4 GPa) and more uniform (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0004">4b</a> and S17b), which is more conducive to resisting external force.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0031_R_d8816135e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> Corresponding to this, it can be observed that the thickness of the SEI on the surface of the HC anode is non-uniform (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0004">4c</a>), which is caused by continuous decomposition and accumulation of carbonate solvent molecules. In contrast, a uniform and conformal SEI is constructed on the surface of TMS-HC (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0004">4d</a>). These results indicate that the SEI endowed by the regulated interphase is more robust and homogeneous, contributing to the stable and fast transfer of Na<sup>+</sup>.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202424028-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ae49756b-33b4-42e3-9b8f-a36abaff5531/anie202424028-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ae49756b-33b4-42e3-9b8f-a36abaff5531/anie202424028-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/667eb8f2-e5d2-4a15-9a29-c6a17d10ab1b/anie202424028-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ae49756b-33b4-42e3-9b8f-a36abaff5531/anie202424028-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202424028-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202424028" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Chemical components of SEI on the surface of HC and HC-TMS anodes. Modulus distribution of HC surface (a) and TMS-HC surface (b). TEM images of HC (c) and TMS-HC (d). Depth-profiling XPS spectra of C 1s (e), F 1s (f), and O 1s (g) of the SEI on the HC surface and C 1s (h), F 1s (i), and O 1s (j) of TMS-HC surface. 2D (k) and 3D (l) spatial distribution of NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, and C<sub>2</sub>H<sup>−</sup> in the SEI of HC and TMS-HC anodes.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The chemical components of SEI are important feedback for interphase chemistry regulation. Therefore, the depth profiles of SEI components are further analyzed by conducting an in-depth Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering for 0 s, 30 s, and 60 s with an etching rate of approximately 0.2 nm s<sup>−1</sup>. For the SEI of HC (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0004">4e</a>–<a href="#anie202424028-fig-0004">g</a>), it can be observed that the chemical species are distributed unevenly in the inner and outer layers of SEI. Visibly, organic species, such as C−O, R−O-Na, etc., have a high content in the outer layer of SEI, which indicates that the organic species continuously accumulate on the surface of HC. However, the inorganic species, especially NaF, have a low proportion in the outer layer of SEI. As a result, uneven distribution of chemical components in the SEI layer can easily lead to unbalanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion through the SEI layer, thereby reducing the cycling life and rate capacity of the HC anode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0034_R_d8816135e1212" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">14</a></sup></span> In contrast, with the sputtering time increasing, no obvious change of some organic species (C−O, O−C=O, etc.) can be observed in the SEI of TMS-HC (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0004">4h</a>–<a href="#anie202424028-fig-0004">j</a>). Furthermore, inorganic species such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF are evenly distributed in the inner and outer layers of SEI. Despite the appearance of PO<sub>x</sub>F<sub>y</sub> in the outermost layer of SEI, NaF still accounts for a significant portion of the content, which is conducive to Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion through the SEI.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0035_R_d8816135e1232" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">15</a></sup></span> The components of the SEI are further confirmed by time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS). The 2D spatial distribution of the cycled anodes displays stronger signals of NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaO<sup>−</sup>, and organic components C<sub>2</sub>H<sup>−</sup> (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0004">4k</a> and S18). Compared with the SEI of HC, the SEI of TMS-HC possesses a relatively more uniform distribution of chemical components on the surface, which is more conducive to the formation of uniform Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion flux.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0036_R_d8816135e1253" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Nano">16</a></sup></span> The 3D views of NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaO<sup>−</sup>, and organic components C<sub>2</sub>H<sup>−</sup> are displayed in Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0004">4l</a> and S19 and the overlay 3D pictures are displayed in Figure S20. Obviously, the distribution of inorganic components in the entire space is abnormally uneven in the SEI of the HC anode, while inorganic and organic components are evenly distributed in the SEI of the TMS-HC anode corresponding to the intensity sputtering profiles (Figure S21), which demonstrates the enrichment effect of the Si−O−Si molecular layer on anions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0037_R_d8816135e1273" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">17</a></sup></span> Organic components (C<sub>2</sub>H<sup>−</sup>, etc) can alleviate the stress caused by volume expansion and inhibit harmful side reactions, while inorganic components (NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, etc) can improve the mechanical strength of SEI and promote ion transport, so the uniform distribution of organic and inorganic species can offer good protection for the TMS-HC anode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0034_R_d8816135e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">14</a>, <a href="#anie202424028-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0038_R_d8816135e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span> In summary, the results of XPS and TOF-SIMS consistently reflect that the distribution of organic and inorganic chemical components in the SEI layer of TMS-HC is more uniform, revealing the important role of the Si−O−Si molecular layer in regulating interphase chemistry.</p>
            
            <p>The evolution of the Si−O−Si molecular layer during the formation of SEI is further analyzed. The SEI chemical components on the TMS-HC surface are studied by cryogenic transmission electron microscopy (cryo-TEM, Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0005">5a</a>) to achieve the stability of the SEI layer under air exposure. The cryo-TEM image and corresponding element mapping indicate solvent molecules and anions synergistically participate in the formation of the SEI layer (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0005">5b</a> and S22). Notably, Si element mapping shows the Si−O−Si molecular layer did not detach during the formation of SEI, but instead integrated into the SEI layer (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0005">5b</a><sub><b>2</b></sub>), in which the local molecular layer and electrolyte components jointly participate in the formation process of SEI to form Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0005">5c</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0041_R_d8816135e1309" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Environ. Sci.">19</a></sup></span> In addition, chemical components of NaF can be observed in the inner layer of SEI, indicating that the molecular layer does not hinder Na<sup>+</sup> transport and charge transfer. Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0005">5d</a> displays the distribution of Si species in the SEI layer. As the etching time increases, the signal of components containing Si becomes stronger and stronger, indicating the Si−O−Si molecular layer is embedded in the inner layer of the SEI. Moreover, most of the molecular layer still maintain their original chemical components, while some molecular layers cooperate with anions or solvents to form chemical components.<span><sup><a href="#anie202424028-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202424028-bib-0042_R_d8816135e1317" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Environ. Sci. Technol.">20</a></sup></span> From the depth profiles and corresponding 3D distribution (Figures <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0005">5e</a> and <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0005">f</a>), numerous components containing Si are distributed in the inner SEI layer, while it is rarely distributed in the outer SEI layer, which is consistent with the XPS results. Based on the above results, the SEI formed on the surface of HC and TMS-HC anodes can be simplified into the following model (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0005">5g</a>). For the SEI on the HC surface, plenty of organic components derived from solvents fill the entire SEI layer, while inorganic components derived from anions (such as NaF) are less and unevenly distributed, which leads to unstable Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the SEI layer. For the SEI on the TMS-HC surface, driven by the strong ion-dipole interaction between Si−O−Si and anions, organic and inorganic components are uniformly distributed, contributing to stable Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion through SEI. Moreover, the retained Si−O−Si molecular layer can serve as the skeleton of SEI, supporting its stable operation.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202424028-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6dc77702-fbc8-4c60-8d84-9fe42c491c96/anie202424028-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6dc77702-fbc8-4c60-8d84-9fe42c491c96/anie202424028-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/67e869b8-7c0f-4ac9-999f-32f57324a114/anie202424028-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6dc77702-fbc8-4c60-8d84-9fe42c491c96/anie202424028-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202424028-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202424028" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Evolution of the Si−O−Si molecular layer. (a) Schematic illustration of SEI chemical composition analyzed by Cryo-TEM. (b) Low magnification cryo-TEM image of the TMS-HC (b<sub>1</sub>) and Si element mapping (b<sub>2</sub>). (c) Higher-magnification view of the SEI on the TMS-HC surface (c<sub>1</sub>) and corresponding FFTs (c<sub>2</sub>). (d) Depth-profiling XPS spectra of Si 2p.                   </figcaption>

            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202424028-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202424028-sec-0007-title"> Practical Application and Large-Scale Preparation of TMS-HC Anode</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the practical performance of the TMS-HC anode, the coin-type full-cell and pouch-cell with Fe-PB cathode are operated. After cycling in half-cell for 10 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, HC and TMS-HC anodes are reassembled into coin-type full-cell with Fe-PB cathode. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0006">6a</a>, the ICE of TMS-HC || Fe-PB coin-type full-cell is 96.4 % with a specific capacity of 140.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, while the ICE of HC || Fe-PB coin-type full-cell is 95.9 % with a specific capacity of 136.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Impressively, the TMS-HC || Fe-PB coin-type full-cell delivers significantly excellent capacity retention of 80.9 % after 800 cycles and 74.2 % after 1000 cycles at 1C (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0006">6b</a>, <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0001">1C</a>=170 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), while the specific capacity of HC || Fe-PB coin-type full-cell decreases to 85.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles with a low capacity retention of 70.3 %. In addition, the TMS-HC || Fe-PB coin-type full-cell exhibits excellent rate capability (Figure S23). Furthermore, the 100 mAh pouch-cells were prepared, and their structure is shown in Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0006">6c</a> (inset). Obviously, the TMS-HC || Fe-PB pouch-cell exhibits higher discharging capacity and cycling stability (Figures S24, 6c, and 6d). Moreover, even at −20 °C, the TMS-HC || Fe-PB pouch-cell still has a high discharging capacity (Figure S25, &gt;70 % of capacity at room temperature (RT)) and excellent cycling stability (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0006">6e</a>). In addition, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0006">6f</a>, the “HIT” LEDs are lit by TMS-HC || Fe-PB pouch-cell at RT and −20 °C, which indicates that the TMS-HC anode exhibits attractive commercialization prospects. Specifically, the large-scale preparation schematic of TMS-HC anodes (Figure <a href="#anie202424028-fig-0006">6g</a>) shows the great potential of the targeted functionalization strategy of HC in the commercial preparation of SIBs and its promotion process in high-capacity SIBs still needs the efforts of more researchers.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202424028-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5ded6529-5e02-48c2-a8f6-37bfd256faa3/anie202424028-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5ded6529-5e02-48c2-a8f6-37bfd256faa3/anie202424028-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/18257008-821c-4633-9c5a-b31c51df22eb/anie202424028-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5ded6529-5e02-48c2-a8f6-37bfd256faa3/anie202424028-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202424028-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202424028" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performance of coin-type full-cell and pouch-cell. (a) GCD curves of the initial cycle at 0.1C of assembled coin-type full-cell after anodes’ cycling in the half-cell for 10 cycles. (b) Long-term cycling performance of coin-type full-cell at 1C (1C=170 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). (c) GCD curves of the 100 mAh pouch-cell. (d) Long-term cycling performance of 100 mAh pouch-cell at 0.5C. (e) Long-term cycling performance of the TMS-HC || Fe-PB pouch-cell at 0.5C in −20 °C. (f) An optical photograph of the TMS-HC || Fe-PB pouch-cell lits LEDs. (g) Schematic illustration of the large-scale preparation of TMS-HC anode.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202424028-sec-0008">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202424028-sec-0008-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the interphase chemistry of HC anode in ester-based electrolytes is regulated effectively by targeted functional groups of the HC surface, which enhances the enrichment of anions at the anode/electrolyte interface, further constructing the highly durable and sturdy organic/inorganic hybrid SEI. Therefore, the stability of the HC anode in the ester-based electrolytes is improved and the activation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the interphase is decreased significantly. Specifically, the modified HC anode (TMS-HC) possesses an excellent rate capability of 206.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and outstanding cycling performance of 92.5 % capacity retention after 1000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and the TMS-HC || Fe-PB coin-type full-cell delivers excellent capacity retention ratio of 80.9 % after 800 cycles at 1C. More importantly, the TMS-HC anode exhibits significant potential for attractive commercialization prospects and convenient large-scale preparation. This work demonstrates that fine chemical regulation between surface functional groups and electrolyte components has undeniable roles in improving the SEI durability of HC anode in ester-based electrolytes for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
